         <span class="play-btn-large">
          <span class="mw-tmh-playtext">
           Play media
          </span>
         </span>
        </a>
       </div>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Correcta_posici%C3%B3n_para_la_toma_de_presi%C3%B3n_arterial.webm" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Right position for taking blood pressure
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Arterial pressure is most commonly measured via a
      <a href="/wiki/Sphygmomanometer" title="Sphygmomanometer">
       sphygmomanometer
      </a>
      , which historically used the height of a column of mercury to reflect the circulating pressure.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Booth1977_44-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Booth1977-44">
        [44]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Blood pressure values are generally reported in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Millimetre_of_mercury" title="Millimetre of mercury">
       millimetres of mercury
      </a>
      (mmHg), though aneroid and electronic devices do not contain
      <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">
       mercury
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      For each heartbeat, blood pressure varies between systolic and diastolic pressures. Systolic pressure is peak pressure in the arteries, which occurs near the end of the
      <a href="/wiki/Cardiac_cycle" title="Cardiac cycle">
       cardiac cycle
      </a>
      when the
      <a href="/wiki/Ventricle_(heart)" title="Ventricle (heart)">
       ventricles
      </a>
      are contracting. Diastolic pressure is minimum pressure in the arteries, which occurs near the beginning of the cardiac cycle when the ventricles are filled with blood. An example of normal measured values for a resting, healthy adult human is 120 mmHg
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Systole_(medicine)" title="Systole (medicine)">
       systolic
      </a>
      and 80 mmHg
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diastolic" title="Diastolic">
       diastolic
      </a>
      (written as 120/80 mmHg, and spoken as "one-twenty over eighty").
     </p>
     <p>
      Systolic and diastolic arterial blood pressures are not static but undergo natural variations from one heartbeat to another and throughout the day (in a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circadian" title="Circadian">
       circadian
      </a>
      rhythm). They also change in response to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stress_(medicine)" title="Stress (medicine)">
       stress
      </a>
      , nutritional factors,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medication" title="Medication">
       drugs
      </a>
      , disease, exercise, and
      <a href="/wiki/Orthostatic_hypotension" title="Orthostatic hypotension">
       momentarily from standing up
      </a>
      . Sometimes the variations are large.
      <a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">
       Hypertension
      </a>
      refers to arterial pressure being abnormally high, as opposed to
      <a href="/wiki/Hypotension" title="Hypotension">
       hypotension
      </a>
      , when it is abnormally low. Along with
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Body_temperature" title="Body temperature">
       body temperature
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_rate" title="Respiratory rate">
       respiratory rate
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulse_rate" title="Pulse rate">
       pulse rate
      </a>
      , blood pressure is one of the four main vital signs routinely monitored by medical professionals and healthcare providers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OHSU_45-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-OHSU-45">
        [45]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Measuring pressure
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Invasive_blood_pressure" title="Invasive blood pressure">
       invasively
      </a>
      , by penetrating the arterial wall to take the measurement, is much less common and usually restricted to a hospital setting.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Non-invasive">
       Non-invasive
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Blood_pressure&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Non-invasive">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The non-invasive
      <a href="/wiki/Auscultation" title="Auscultation">
       auscultatory
      </a>
      and oscillometric measurements are simpler and quicker than invasive measurements, require less expertise, have virtually no complications, are less unpleasant and less painful for the patient. However, non-invasive methods may yield somewhat lower accuracy and small systematic differences in numerical results. Non-invasive measurement methods are more commonly used for routine examinations and monitoring.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Palpation">
       Palpation
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Blood_pressure&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Palpation">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      A minimum systolic value can be roughly estimated by
      <a href="/wiki/Palpation" title="Palpation">
       palpation
      </a>
      , most often used in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emergency_medical_service" title="Emergency medical service">
       emergency situations
      </a>
      , but should be used with caution.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deakin2000_46-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Deakin2000-46">
        [46]
       </a>
      </sup>
      It has been estimated that, using 50%
      <a href="/wiki/Percentile" title="Percentile">
       percentiles
      </a>
      , carotid, femoral and radial pulses are present in patients with a systolic blood pressure &gt; 70 mmHg, carotid and femoral pulses alone in patients with systolic blood pressure of &gt; 50 mmHg, and only a carotid pulse in patients with a systolic blood pressure of &gt; 40 mmHg.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Deakin2000_46-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Deakin2000-46">
        [46]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A more accurate value of systolic blood pressure can be obtained with a
      <a href="/wiki/Sphygmomanometer" title="Sphygmomanometer">
       sphygmomanometer
      </a>
      and palpating the radial pulse.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The diastolic blood pressure cannot be estimated by this method. The American Heart Association recommends that palpation be used to get an estimate before using the auscultatory method.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Auscultatory">
       Auscultatory
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Blood_pressure&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Auscultatory">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sphygmomanometer.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="376" data-file-width="549" height="151" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Sphygmomanometer.jpg/220px-Sphygmomanometer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Sphygmomanometer.jpg/330px-Sphygmomanometer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Sphygmomanometer.jpg/440px-Sphygmomanometer.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sphygmomanometer.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Auscultatory method aneroid sphygmomanometer with stethoscope
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mercury_manometer.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="558" data-file-width="500" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Mercury_manometer.jpg/220px-Mercury_manometer.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Mercury_manometer.jpg/330px-Mercury_manometer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Mercury_manometer.jpg/440px-Mercury_manometer.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mercury_manometer.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Mercury manometer
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The auscultatory method (from the Latin word for "listening") uses a
      <a href="/wiki/Stethoscope" title="Stethoscope">
       stethoscope
      </a>
      and a
      <a href="/wiki/Sphygmomanometer" title="Sphygmomanometer">
       sphygmomanometer
      </a>
      . This comprises an inflatable (
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Scipione_Riva-Rocci" title="Scipione Riva-Rocci">
        Riva-Rocci
       </a>
      </i>
      )
      <a href="/wiki/Cuff" title="Cuff">
       cuff
      </a>
      placed around the upper
      <a href="/wiki/Arm" title="Arm">
       arm
      </a>
      at roughly the same vertical height as the heart, attached to a mercury or
      <a href="/wiki/Pressure_measurement#Aneroid" title="Pressure measurement">
       aneroid
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manometer" title="Manometer">
       manometer
      </a>
      . The mercury manometer, considered the gold standard, measures the height of a column of mercury, giving an absolute result without need for calibration and, consequently, not subject to the errors and drift of calibration which affect other methods. The use of mercury manometers is often required in
      <a href="/wiki/Clinical_trial" title="Clinical trial">
       clinical trials
      </a>
      and for the clinical measurement of
      <a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">
       hypertension
      </a>
      in high-risk patients, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">
       pregnant women
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      A cuff of the appropriate size
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cuff-size_48-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-cuff-size-48">
        [48]
       </a>
      </sup>
      is fitted smoothly and also snugly, then inflated manually by repeatedly squeezing a rubber bulb until the artery is completely occluded. It is important that the cuff size is correct: undersized cuffs record too high a pressure; oversized cuffs may yield too low a pressure.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OBrien2003_49-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-OBrien2003-49">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Usually three or four cuff sizes should be available to allow measurements in arms of different size.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OBrien2003_49-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-OBrien2003-49">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Listening with the stethoscope to the
      <a href="/wiki/Brachial_artery" title="Brachial artery">
       brachial artery
      </a>
      at the
      <a href="/wiki/Cubital_fossa" title="Cubital fossa">
       antecubital area
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Elbow" title="Elbow">
       elbow
      </a>
      , the examiner slowly releases the pressure in the cuff. When blood just starts to flow in the artery, the
      <a href="/wiki/Turbulence" title="Turbulence">
       turbulent flow
      </a>
      creates a "whooshing" or pounding (first
      <a href="/wiki/Korotkoff_sounds" title="Korotkoff sounds">
       Korotkoff sound
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        [50]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The pressure at which this sound is first heard is the systolic blood pressure. The cuff pressure is further released until no sound can be heard (fifth Korotkoff sound), at the diastolic arterial pressure.
     </p>
     <p>
      The auscultatory method is the predominant method of clinical measurement.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pickering2005p146_51-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pickering2005p146-51">
        [51]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Oscillometric">
       Oscillometric
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Blood_pressure&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Oscillometric">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      The oscillometric method was first demonstrated in 1876 and involves the observation of oscillations in the sphygmomanometer cuff pressure
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pickering2005p147_52-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pickering2005p147-52">
        [52]
       </a>
      </sup>
      which are caused by the oscillations of
      <a href="/wiki/Blood_flow" title="Blood flow">
       blood flow
      </a>
      , i.e., the
      <a href="/wiki/Pulse" title="Pulse">
       pulse
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laurent2003_53-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Laurent2003-53">
        [53]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The electronic version of this method is sometimes used in long-term measurements and general practice. It uses a sphygmomanometer cuff, like the auscultatory method, but with an electronic
      <a href="/wiki/Pressure_sensor" title="Pressure sensor">
       pressure sensor
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Transducer" title="Transducer">
       transducer
      </a>
      ) to observe cuff pressure oscillations, electronics to automatically interpret them, and automatic inflation and deflation of the cuff. The pressure sensor should be calibrated periodically to maintain accuracy.
     </p>
     <p>
      Oscillometric measurement requires less skill than the auscultatory technique and may be suitable for use by untrained staff and for automated patient home monitoring. As for the auscultatory technique it is important that the cuff size is appropriate for the arm. There are some single cuff devices that may be used for arms of differing sizes, although experience with these is limited.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OBrien2003_49-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-OBrien2003-49">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The cuff is inflated to a pressure initially in excess of the systolic arterial pressure and then reduced to below diastolic pressure over a period of about 30 seconds. When blood flow is nil (cuff pressure exceeding systolic pressure) or unimpeded (cuff pressure below diastolic pressure), cuff pressure will be essentially constant. When blood flow is present, but restricted, the cuff pressure, which is monitored by the pressure sensor, will vary periodically in synchrony with the cyclic expansion and contraction of the brachial artery, i.e., it will
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oscillate" title="Oscillate">
       oscillate
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Over the deflation period, the recorded pressure waveform forms a signal known as the cuff deflation curve. A bandpass filter is utilized to extract the oscillometric pulses from the cuff deflation curve. Over the deflation period, the extracted oscillometric pulses form a signal known as the oscillometric waveform (OMW). The amplitude of the oscillometric pulses increases to a maximum and then decreases with further deflation. A variety of analysis algorithms can be employed in order to estimate the systolic, diastolic, and mean arterial pressure.
     </p>
     <p>
      Oscillometric monitors may produce inaccurate readings in patients with heart and circulation problems, which include
      <a href="/wiki/Arteriosclerosis" title="Arteriosclerosis">
       arteriosclerosis
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arrhythmia" title="Arrhythmia">
       arrhythmia
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Preeclampsia" title="Preeclampsia">
       preeclampsia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Pulsus_alternans" title="Pulsus alternans">
       pulsus alternans
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Pulsus_paradoxus" title="Pulsus paradoxus">
       pulsus paradoxus
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OBrien2003_49-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-OBrien2003-49">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        [54]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In practice the different methods do not give identical results; an algorithm and experimentally obtained coefficients are used to adjust the oscillometric results to give readings which match the auscultatory results as well as possible. Some equipment uses computer-aided analysis of the instantaneous arterial pressure
      <a href="/wiki/Waveform" title="Waveform">
       waveform
      </a>
      to determine the systolic, mean, and diastolic points. Since many oscillometric devices have not been validated, caution must be given as most are not suitable in clinical and acute care settings.
     </p>
     <p>
      Recently, several coefficient-free oscillometric algorithms have developed for estimation of blood pressure. These algorithms do not rely on experimentally obtained coefficients and have been shown to provide more accurate and robust estimation of blood pressure. These algorithms are based on finding the fundamental relationship between the oscillometric waveform and the blood pressure using modeling
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        [55]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        [56]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and learning
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
      approaches.
     </p>
     <p>
      The term NIBP, for non-invasive blood pressure, is often used to describe oscillometric monitoring equipment.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Continuous_noninvasive_techniques_.28CNAP.29">
       Continuous noninvasive techniques (CNAP)
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Blood_pressure&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Continuous noninvasive techniques (CNAP)">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Continuous_Noninvasive_Arterial_Pressure" title="Continuous Noninvasive Arterial Pressure">
       Continuous Noninvasive Arterial Pressure
      </a>
      (CNAP) is the method of measuring arterial blood pressure in real-time without any interruptions and without cannulating the human body. CNAP combines the advantages of the following two clinical “gold standards”: it measures blood pressure continuously in real-time like the invasive
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arterial_catheter" title="Arterial catheter">
       arterial catheter
      </a>
      system and it is noninvasive like the standard upper arm
      <a href="/wiki/Sphygmomanometer" title="Sphygmomanometer">
       sphygmomanometer
      </a>
      . Latest developments in this field show promising results in terms of accuracy, ease of use and clinical acceptance.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Non-occlusive_techniques:_The_Pulse_Wave_Velocity_.28PWV.29_principle">
       Non-occlusive techniques: The Pulse Wave Velocity (PWV) principle
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Blood_pressure&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Non-occlusive techniques: The Pulse Wave Velocity (PWV) principle">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      Since the 1990s a novel family of techniques based on the so-called
      <a href="/wiki/Pulse_wave_velocity" title="Pulse wave velocity">
       pulse wave velocity
      </a>
      (PWV) principle have been developed. These techniques rely on the fact that the velocity at which an arterial pressure pulse travels along the arterial tree depends, among others, on the underlying blood pressure.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Accordingly, after a calibration maneuver, these techniques provide indirect estimates of blood pressure by translating PWV values into blood pressure values.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The main advantage of these techniques is that it is possible to measure PWV values of a subject continuously (beat-by-beat), without medical supervision, and without the need of inflating brachial cuffs. PWV-based techniques are still in the research domain and are not adapted to clinical settings.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Ambulatory_and_home_monitoring">
       Ambulatory and home monitoring
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Blood_pressure&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Ambulatory and home monitoring">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Ambulatory_blood_pressure" title="Ambulatory blood pressure">
       Ambulatory blood pressure
      </a>
      devices take readings regularly (e.g. every half-hour throughout the day and night). They have been used to exclude measurement problems like
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White-coat_hypertension" title="White-coat hypertension">
       white-coat hypertension
      </a>
      and provide more reliable estimates of usual blood pressure and cardiovascular risk. Blood pressure readings outside of a clinical setting are usually slightly lower in the majority of people; however studies that quantified the risks from
      <a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">
       hypertension
      </a>
      and the benefits of lowering blood pressure have mostly been based on readings in a clinical environment. Use of ambulatory measurements is not widespread but guidelines developed by the UK National Institute for Health and Care Excellence and the British Hypertension Society recommended that 24-hour ambulatory blood pressure monitoring should be used for diagnosis of hypertension.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NICEBHS2011_60-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NICEBHS2011-60">
        [60]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Health economic analysis suggested that this approach would be cost effective compared with repeated clinic measurements.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61">
       <a href="#cite_note-61">
        [61]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Home monitoring is a cheap and simple alternative to ambulatory blood pressure monitoring, although it does not usually allow assessment of blood pressure during sleep which may be a disadvantage.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mancia2007_62-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Mancia2007-62">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pickering2008_63-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pickering2008-63">
        [63]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Automatic self-contained blood pressure monitors are available at reasonable prices, however measurements may not be accurate in patients with atrial fibrillation or other arrhythmias such as frequent ectopic beats.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mancia2007_62-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Mancia2007-62">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pickering2008_63-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pickering2008-63">
        [63]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Home monitoring may be used to improve hypertension management and to monitor the effects of lifestyle changes and medication related to blood pressure.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chobanian2003_3-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Chobanian2003-3">
        [3]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Compared to ambulatory blood pressure measurements, home monitoring has been found to be an effective and lower cost alternative,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mancia2007_62-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Mancia2007-62">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Niiranen2006_64-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Niiranen2006-64">
        [64]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Shimbo2007_65-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Shimbo2007-65">
        [65]
       </a>
      </sup>
      but ambulatory monitoring is more accurate than both clinic and home monitoring in diagnosing hypertension.
     </p>
     <p>
      When measuring blood pressure in the home, an accurate reading requires that one not drink coffee, smoke cigarettes, or engage in strenuous exercise for 30 minutes before taking the reading. A full bladder may have a small effect on blood pressure readings; if the urge to urinate arises, one should do so before the reading. For 5 minutes before the reading, one should sit upright in a chair with one's feet flat on the floor and with limbs uncrossed. The blood pressure cuff should always be against bare skin, as readings taken over a shirt sleeve are less accurate. The same arm should be used for all measurements. During the reading, the arm that is used should be relaxed and kept at heart level, for example by resting it on a table.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        [66]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Since blood pressure varies throughout the day, home measurements should be taken at the same time of day. A Joint Scientific Statement From the American Heart Association, American Society of Hypertension, and Preventive Cardiovascular Nurses Association on home monitoring in 2008
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pickering2008_63-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pickering2008-63">
        [63]
       </a>
      </sup>
      recommended that 2 to 3 readings should be taken in the morning (after awakening, before washing/dressing, taking breakfast/drink or taking medication) and another 2 to 3 readings at night, each day over a period of 1 week. It was also recommended that the readings from the first day should be discarded and that a total of ≥12 readings (i.e. at least two readings per day for the remaining 6 days of the week) should be used for making clinical decisions.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="White-coat_hypertension">
       White-coat hypertension
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Blood_pressure&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: White-coat hypertension">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      For some patients, blood pressure measurements taken in a doctor's office may not correctly characterize their typical blood pressure.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Elliot2007_67-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Elliot2007-67">
        [67]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In up to 25% of patients, the office measurement is higher than their typical blood pressure. This type of error is called
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White-coat_hypertension" title="White-coat hypertension">
       white-coat hypertension
      </a>
      (WCH) and can result from anxiety related to an examination by a health care professional.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jhalani2005_68-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Jhalani2005-68">
        [68]
       </a>
      </sup>
      White coat hypertension can also occur because, in a clinical setting, patients are seldom given the opportunity to rest for five minutes before blood pressure readings are taken. The misdiagnosis of hypertension for these patients can result in needless and possibly harmful medication. WCH can be reduced (but not eliminated) with automated blood pressure measurements over 15 to 20 minutes in a quiet part of the office or clinic.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pickering2005p145_69-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pickering2005p145-69">
        [69]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In some cases a lower blood pressure reading occurs at the doctor's - this has been termed 'masked hypertension'.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pickering2005p146a_70-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Pickering2005p146a-70">
        [70]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
      <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
       <div style="width: 155px">
        <div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">
         <div style="margin:30px auto;">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blood_Pressure_-_Take_Another_Person.png">

          </a>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="gallerytext">
         <p>
          Blood Pressure – Take another person.
         </p>
        </div>
       </div>
      </li>
      <li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
       <div style="width: 155px">
        <div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">
         <div style="margin:34.5px auto;">
          <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blood_Pressure_-_Take_Your_Own.png">

          </a>
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="gallerytext">
         <p>
          Blood Pressure – Take your own.
         </p>
        </div>
       </div>
      </li>
     </ul>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Invasive">
       Invasive
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Blood_pressure&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Invasive">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Arterial blood pressure is most accurately measured invasively through an
      <a href="/wiki/Arterial_line" title="Arterial line">
       arterial line
      </a>
      . Invasive arterial pressure measurement with intravascular
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cannulae" title="Cannulae">
       cannulae
      </a>
      involves direct measurement of arterial pressure by placing a cannula needle in an artery (usually
      <a href="/wiki/Radial_artery" title="Radial artery">
       radial
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Femoral_artery" title="Femoral artery">
       femoral
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Dorsalis_pedis_artery" title="Dorsalis pedis artery">
       dorsalis pedis
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Brachial_artery" title="Brachial artery">
       brachial
      </a>
      ).
     </p>
     <p>
      The cannula must be connected to a sterile, fluid-filled system, which is connected to an electronic pressure transducer. The advantage of this system is that pressure is constantly monitored beat-by-beat, and a waveform (a graph of pressure against time) can be displayed. This invasive technique is regularly employed in human and veterinary
      <a href="/wiki/Intensive_care_medicine" title="Intensive care medicine">
       intensive care medicine
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Anesthesiology" title="Anesthesiology">
       anesthesiology
      </a>
      , and for research purposes.
     </p>
     <p>
      Cannulation for invasive vascular pressure monitoring is infrequently associated with complications such as
      <a href="/wiki/Thrombosis" title="Thrombosis">
       thrombosis
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Infection" title="Infection">
       infection
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemorrhage" title="Hemorrhage">
       bleeding
      </a>
      . Patients with invasive arterial monitoring require very close supervision, as there is a danger of severe bleeding if the line becomes disconnected. It is generally reserved for patients where rapid variations in arterial pressure are anticipated.
     </p>
     <p>
      Invasive vascular pressure monitors are pressure monitoring systems designed to acquire pressure information for display and processing. There are a variety of invasive vascular pressure monitors for trauma, critical care, and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Operating_theatre" title="Operating theatre">
       operating room
      </a>
      applications. These include single pressure, dual pressure, and multi-parameter (i.e. pressure / temperature). The monitors can be used for measurement and follow-up of arterial, central venous, pulmonary arterial, left atrial, right atrial, femoral arterial, umbilical venous, umbilical arterial, and intracranial pressures.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Fetal_blood_pressure">
       Fetal blood pressure
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Blood_pressure&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Fetal blood pressure">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Further information:
      <a href="/wiki/Fetal_circulation#Blood_pressure" title="Fetal circulation">
       Fetal circulation § Blood pressure
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      In
      <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">
       pregnancy
      </a>
      , it is the fetal heart and not the mother's heart that builds up the fetal blood pressure to drive blood through the fetal circulation. The blood pressure in the fetal aorta is approximately 30 mmHg at 20 weeks of gestation, and increases to approximately 45 mmHg at 40 weeks of gestation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Struijk_71-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Struijk-71">
        [71]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The average blood pressure for full-term infants:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SharonSmithMurray_72-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-SharonSmithMurray-72">
        [72]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <ul>
      <li>
       Systolic 65–95 mmHg
      </li>
      <li>
       Diastolic 30–60 mmHg
      </li>
     </ul>

        Pickering TG, Hall JE, Appel LJ, et al. (2005). Subcommittee of Professional Public Education of the American Heart Association Council on High Blood Pressure Research.
        <a class="external text" href="http://hyper.ahajournals.org/cgi/content/full/45/1/142" rel="nofollow">
         "Recommendations for blood pressure measurement in humans and experimental animals: Part 1: blood pressure measurement in humans: a statement for professionals from the Subcommittee of Professional and Public Education of the American Heart Association Council on High Blood Pressure Research"
        </a>
        .
        <i>
         Hypertension
        </i>
        .
        <b>
         45
        </b>
        (5): 142–61.
        <a href="/wiki/Digital_object_identifier" title="Digital object identifier">
         doi
        </a>

        <a class="external text" href="//dx.doi.org/10.1161%2F01.HYP.0000150859.47929.8e" rel="nofollow">
         10.1161/01.HYP.0000150859.47929.8e
        </a>
        .
